# Chapter 1

## CHAPTER 1

Marlowe’s apartment was a cathedral of unfinished things: a half-knotted macramé wall-hanging wept threads onto a stack of unread Simone de Beauvoir, a forgotten stick of palo santo smoldered in a scallop shell, and three separate tarot decks fanned across the floor like fallen wings. She was trying to read them all at once.



“That’s the cheap stuff, mija. You want the one with the worm. More bang for your felony.”

I froze. The voice was a rustle of taffeta, a crackle of static from an old radio. I knew it without turning. Abuela Celia, dead seven years, standing by the canned beans in her favorite mint-green housecoat and slippers with pom-poms.

I let go of the bottle. It thunked against the shelf but didn’t break. I turned slowly, my heart a trapped bird against my ribs.

She was translucent, a watercolor version of herself. The fluorescent lights passed right through the set of her jaw, the familiar disapproval in her arched brows. “What?” she said, gesturing with a spectral hand. “You think death is an excuse to forget standards?”

“You’re not here,” I whispered to the Goya black beans.

“And you’re not a shoplifter, but here we are.” She floated closer. “Look at you. Twenty-four years old. Your hair is a nest of disappointed birds. You smell of subway and regret. And you’re stealing the wrong mezcal.”

This was it. The final crack in the dam. I’d been holding the city at bay with spit and frayed wire for months—the eviction notice folded like a secret in my back pocket, the temp agency’s silence, the way my own reflection in the train windows seemed to blur at the edges, like I was fading from the picture. And now my dead grandmother was giving me liquor advice in the bodega.

“Go away,” I hissed, grabbing the bottle with the worm. I didn’t care anymore. I’d pay or I’d run. Either was preferable to a spiritual intervention in the snack aisle.

“Make me,” Abuela Celia said, and crossed her arms. The pom-poms on her slippers jiggled.

I strode to the counter, slammed the bottle down. The old man reading *El Diario* didn’t look up. He rang it up, I handed over the last of my cash, and I walked out into the grimy afternoon, the ghost gliding beside me.

“You’re not real,” I said, marching west.

“And yet I have opinions on your life choices. A paradox.”

“You’re a stress-induced hallucination. A psychotic break with a Cuban accent.”

“A break would be a vacation, *corazón*. This is an intervention.” She kept pace, her form flickering as we passed under the skeletal shadows of fire escapes. “Your mother is worried.”

I barked a laugh. “My mother thinks I’m a failed experiment. She told me last week that my aura was the color of unfulfilled potential. She smudged my apartment with sage and called it ‘career counseling.’”

“Your mother sees the world through a prism I gave her,” Abuela said, her voice softening. “A prism she refused to polish. So it shows her only fractures. But you… you she sees clearly. And it frightens her.”

I stopped at the corner, the light bleeding red. I looked at her, really looked. The details were unbearable: the tiny mole above her lip, the way her left eyebrow had a scar through it from a long-ago fall, the *azabache* bracelet she’d worn against the evil eye, now a shimmer of black on her spectral wrist. Memory couldn’t fabricate this. Memory was a blur. This was HD.

“Why are you here?” The question left me deflated.

The light turned green. The crowd moved around us, through her. A teenager on a bike passed through her stomach; she didn’t flinch.

“The women in our family,” she said, floating beside me as I walked again, “we don’t just see the world. We see the… textures. The layers. Your great-great-grandmother Concha could hear the memories in stones. Your Tía Rosa could taste lies in food. Your mother sees auras, God help her. And you, Valentina…”

“I see dead people. Great. Very original.” I shoved my free hand in my jacket pocket.

“No. You *invite* us.” She said it plainly, a fact. “You’re a beacon. A lighthouse in a very foggy, very crowded spiritual sea. You’ve been shining since you were a girl, but you’ve been closing your eyes. Now you’re desperate. And the door you’ve been leaning against is swinging open.”

We reached my building, a stoop-shouldered tenement with a perpetually weeping facade. I took the steps two at a time, wanting to outrun her. She simply appeared in the dim hallway beside me as I fumbled with my keys.

“I don’t want a beacon. I want a security deposit. I want a job with health insurance. I want to not be having a conversation with a ghost in a hallway that smells of cat pee and boiled cabbage.”

“Wanting is a prayer, Valentina. And your prayers have gotten very, very loud.”

I got the door open, stumbled into my studio. It was one room, a symphony of chaos: books stacked like defensive walls, clothes mounded into soft topographies, a hot plate on the sink, and a futon that never remembered to be a couch. My altar was in the corner—a crowded, dusty affair with a statue of Yemayá, a few dried roses, a fossil, a feminist pin, and a postcard of Frida Kahlo. It was less a spiritual command center and more a junk drawer for my anxieties.

Abuela Celia drifted over to it. She grimaced. “This is a traffic jam. You’ve got an Orisha next to a rock. You’re asking for spiritual cross-talk.”

“It’s decor.”

“It’s a scream into the void, arranged nicely.” She turned to me. “Sit.”

“I’m not doing a séance.”

“Sit. Drink your expensive mezcal. And listen.”

I slumped onto the futon, twisted off the cap, and took a long, burning pull from the bottle. The worm stared up from the bottom, a pale, pickled sentinel. Abuela settled—or gave the impression of settling—onto my one good chair.

“Our line,” she began, “is a line of seekers. Not finders. Seekers. We don’t get answers. We get better questions. We don’t get peace. We get purpose. For generations, the women of our blood have been trying to map the territory between the world you see and the world that is. We use different tools. Santería, espiritismo, feminism, art, pure stubborn rage. They are all lenses. But the vision… the vision is one thing.”

“What vision?”

“That the world is not finished,” she said, her form glowing faintly in the dusk of the room. “It is a verb, not a noun. And women, especially women like us, with one foot here and one… elsewhere… we are the ones who do the work of shaping it. We are the cooks of reality. But the kitchen is on fire, the recipes are written in blood, and everyone is shouting.”

I took another drink. It was making her easier to look at. “That’s not a vision. That’s a panic attack with a metaphor.”

She smiled, a crescent of light. “Exactly. Now you’re getting it. The panic is the first tool. It means you feel the pressure of the unseen. Most people walk through life on a solid floor. We walk on a membrane. And right now, you are feeling it stretch. You are feeling things pressing up from the other side.”

“Ghosts.”

“Ghosts. Memories. Possibilities. The grief of the world. The joy it hasn’t yet had. All of it.” She leaned forward. “Your mother saw it in you young. She tried to bind it, to quiet you with her own fears. She told you the spirits were tricks. That the feelings were anxiety. She gave you pills to dull the beacon.”

A old, familiar anger, warm and bitter, rose in my throat. The little white pills. The therapist who called my “episodes” dissociative. The years of trying to be normal, flat, factual.

“She was trying to protect me.”

“She was trying to protect the membrane,” Abuela corrected. “She was afraid your weight would break it, and everything would spill through. But, *mi vida*, the membrane is already broken. Has been. The spilling is the world. Our job is not to patch it. Our job is to learn to swim in the spill.”

I looked at the bottle, at the worm. A seeker. Not a finder. A swimmer in the spill. The metaphors were piling up, and I was too tired to sort them.

“What do I do?”

“First, you clean this altar. You make a space. Not for one thing. For the everything. You get a bigger table. You listen. And you stop stealing. It upsets the vibrations.”

“That’s your practical advice? Get a bigger table?”

“And pay your rent.” She began to fade, the gardenia scent intensifying. “I will help. I will bring others.”

“Others? What others?”

But she was a wisp, a shimmer of mint-green and pom-poms. “The aunts,” her voice echoed from a growing distance. “They’ve been waiting. The conversation has been so dull without us.”

And she was gone.

The room felt heavy, silent, and profoundly empty. I sat in the gathering dark, the mezcal warm in my belly. I looked at the cluttered altar. A scream into the void, arranged nicely.

I got up. I didn’t turn on the light. I went to the altar and swept everything onto the floor with my arm. The statue, the pins, the dust, the postcard. It fell with a crash and a clatter. I knelt in the debris. I put my forehead on the now-empty surface. The wood was cool.

I didn’t pray. I issued a statement.

“Okay,” I whispered to the empty room, to the membrane, to the spill. “Okay. I’m listening.”

From the street below, a siren wailed. In the wall, a pipe knocked. And in the new, clean space of the altar, a single, impossible gardenia petal appeared, white as a moon, smelling of another world.

# Chapter 2

## CHAPTER 2

The aunts arrived with the rain. Not a gentle rain, but a biblical, sideways downpour that turned the sidewalks into rivers and the gutters into gushing hymns. It was three days after Abuela’s visit. Three days of me trying to explain the ghost to myself while applying for data-entry jobs I knew would never call back. Three days of staring at the empty altar, the gardenia petal now brown and curled, waiting for instructions that didn’t come.

The knock wasn’t a knock. It was a rhythm: *shave-and-a-haircut, two-bits* tattooed on my door with what sounded like a confident fist.

I opened it to a wall of woman.

There were three of them, soaked and magnificent, filling the dank hallway with a humidity of their own. I knew them instantly, from photographs and mother’s hissed stories.

Tía Rosa, the eldest, stood front and center. She was a mountain in a dripping red poncho, her hair a wild corona of silver and black, her eyes the same unforgiving brown as Abuela’s. She held a giant, steaming ceramic pot against her hip like a toddler.

Tía Ife was next, tall and willowy, draped in indigo batik that bled dye in the wet. Dozens of tiny brass bells were braided into her locs, and they gave a soft, constant chime with her slightest movement. She had a leather satchel slung across her body, bulging with unknown contents.

And Tía Lulu, the youngest, a compact fireplug in a yellow slicker and rain boots dotted with cartoon ducks. She held a battered suitcase and a look of unshakable cheer.

“Valentina,” Tía Rosa said, her voice a low cello note. “You’re thinner than the promises of men. Move.”

They swept in, a tide of wet fabric, scent (garlic, patchouli, ozone), and palpable energy. They assessed my studio in one collective glance—the poverty, the chaos, the empty altar.

“This is where the beacon lives?” Tía Ife murmured, the bells whispering. “It’s very… beige.”

“It’s a rental,” I said, defensively.

“So is a coffin,” Tía Rosa said, setting the pot on my hot plate with a thud. She took off her poncho, hung it on a protruding nail. “We’re here to renegotiate the terms. Soup first.”

It wasn’t a request. Tía Lulu was already unpacking bowls from her suitcase—mismatched, chipped, beautiful. Tía Ife was pulling herbs, crystals, and strange root vegetables from her satchel. In minutes, my studio was transformed into a makeshift kitchen and temple. The rain drummed the window, sealing us in.

We ate the soup—a garlicky, golden concoction that tasted of strength and home. No one spoke until the bowls were empty. Tía Rosa wiped her mouth with the back of her hand and fixed me with her gaze.

“Celia says you’re ready. We think you’re drowning. But these are not mutually exclusive.” She lit a cigarillo. The smoke coiled into the damp air. “Your light is on, girl. And you’re trying to hide it under a bushel of eviction notices and existential dread. You can’t. The things that are attracted to it… they don’t care about your credit score.”

“What things?” I asked, the soup warming a cold pit in my stomach.

Tía Ife answered, her voice melodic. “The hungry things. The lonely echoes. The ideas that never got born. The world is thick with them. Most people are opaque. We are… translucent. They see our light. They come to the window.”

“Abuela said I invite them.”

“You do,” Tía Lulu chirped, scrubbing a bowl with fierce efficiency. “By being alive and being one of us. It’s the family business. We’re the ushers for the unfinished symphony.”

I put my head in my hands. “I don’t want this business. I want a business card. A 401k. Silence.”

Tía Rosa blew a smoke ring. “Too late. You were drafted by your DNA. The question is not if you serve. The question is *how*. You can be a competent usher, or you can be a chaotic, messy, brilliant conductor who changes the music. Your grandmother was a conductor. Your mother chose to be an usher, and it has made her small and afraid. You get to pick.”

“What did you pick?” I asked, looking at each of them.

Tía Rosa grinned, a flash of gold. “I run the kitchen. I feed the hungry things. A well-fed spirit is less likely to haunt a mortgage broker or start a poltergeist in a preschool. I cook for the unseen. This is my worship.”

Tía Ife shook her head, bells singing. “I map. I find the places where the membrane is thin. Ley lines in the subway. Echo chambers in old banks. I chart the spiritual geography of the city. I help things find their way home.”

Tía Lulu beamed. “I do PR. I talk to them. Negotiate. Sometimes the haunting is just a misunderstanding. A crossed wire. I’m a spiritual mediator. Also, I bake. A good brownie can solve many cross-dimensional disputes.”

I stared at them. They were serious. Utterly, profoundly, bizarrely serious. And they were here, in my crumbling apartment, as real as the rain.

“Why now?” My voice was small.

Tía Ife’s expression softened. “Because the pressure is building. The collective scream of women right now—it’s not just political. It’s metaphysical. It’s a frequency. It’s thinning the veil everywhere. More lights are turning on. More hungry things are waking up. The old ways, the quiet ushering, aren’t enough. We need more conductors. We need maximalist spirit workers. We need to stop just tending the veil and start *weaving* with it.”

Tía Rosa leaned forward. “Your mother tried to make you quiet. It made you sick. Now, you will learn to make that noise your tool. Your art. Your weapon.”

“How?” The word was a plea.

“Lesson one,” Tía Rosa said, stubbing out her cigarillo. “Grounding. You are a beacon, which means you can attract, but you can also get swept away. You need to be a tree, not a weathervane.” She pointed to the cleared altar space. “That’s not an altar. That’s a workbench. We build your anchor tonight.”

For the next hours, they worked. They instructed me to bring things: something of the earth, something of the water, of fire, of air. Something of my blood. Something I loved without reason.

I brought a stone from the park (earth), a vial of East River water I’d collected in a fit of poetry (water), a lighter (fire), a pigeon feather from the windowsill (air). A lock of my own hair, wrapped in thread (blood). And my tattered, beloved copy of *The Bluest Eye*, which had saved me at sixteen (love).

Under their direction, I didn’t arrange. I built. Tía Rosa guided my hands to crush herbs into the river water. Tía Ife hummed tones that made the air vibrate as I placed the stone. Tía Lulu had me whisper my fears onto the feather and then burn its tip with the lighter, the smell acrid and final.

It was not pretty. It was a messy, potent pile on the corner of the table. It looked like the debris of a small, meaningful explosion.

“Now,” Tía Ife said. “Sit. Put your hands on it. And tell us what you *want*. Not what you need. Not what you fear. What you, Valentina, in your deepest, most untamed heart, *want* for your life.”

I closed my eyes. My hands hovered over the collection of junk and essence. I felt foolish. Then I felt a hum, a warmth rising from the pile. It wasn’t in the room. It was in my palms, my blood.

I didn’t think of safety, or quiet, or a good job.

The words rose, unbidden, from a place I’d kept locked.

“I want to be a verb,” I whispered. “I want to be powerful and kind. I want my voice to make the air shake. I want to understand the spill and love swimming in it. I want to be a sanctuary and a sword. I want to be a legacy, not a liability. I want… I want to be a *conductor*.”

As I spoke, the warmth intensified. The room seemed to hold its breath. The rain slackened.

I opened my eyes.

In the center of the pile, where the lock of my hair lay coiled, a tiny, bright green shoot had pushed through, a single leaf unfurling towards the dim ceiling light.

Tía Lulu clapped her hands, delighted. Tía Ife nodded, a slow, deep smile. Tía Rosa grunted, satisfied.

“Good,” she said. “The anchor takes root. It is connected to your want. That is the strongest tether. Now, it will grow as you do.”

“What happens now?” I asked, staring at the impossible sprout.

“Now,” Tía Rosa said, standing with a groan, “we sleep. Tomorrow, you start your apprenticeship. You have a lot to learn. And the city,” she said, glancing out at the neon-smeared night, “has a lot of ghosts who need more than just an usher.”

They bedded down on my floor with the ease of soldiers, leaving me the futon. I lay in the dark, listening to the symphony of their breathing and the soft chime of Tía Ife’s bells. The anchor hummed on its table, a low, steady pulse I could feel in my teeth.

For the first time in years, I didn’t feel alone. I felt recruited. I felt terrified. I felt, beneath the terror, a thread of something else, something green and pushing upward, like the shoot on the altar.

I was no longer just a woman about to be evicted. I was a verb in training. The thought was so absurd, so immense, that I laughed a single, sharp note into the dark.

From her spot on the floor, Tía Rosa chuckled. “Welcome to the work, *sobrina*. Now shut up and sleep. Tomorrow, we hunt echoes.”

# Chapter 3

## CHAPTER 3

Hunting echoes, it turned out, did not involve ectoplasm or Ouija boards. It involved the 4/5/6 line at the height of rush hour.

“Density,” Tía Ife shouted over the roar of the approaching train, her bells drowned by the screech of brakes. “Spiritual energy follows human energy. Joy, despair, boredom—it all leaves a residue. This station is a palimpsest of panic and hope. Feel it.”

We were packed on the downtown platform at 86th Street, a press of damp wool and exhaustion. I tried to “feel” beyond the immediate assault of body odor and the primal fear of being pushed onto the tracks. I closed my eyes, reaching for the hum I’d felt at the altar.

At first, there was nothing but the subway’s mechanical scream. Then, a layer beneath it: a low, collective thrum of anxiety, a sharper spike of someone’s secret joy, the acid-green smear of someone’s rage. It was like tuning a radio into static and catching fragments of songs.

“There,” Tía Ife said, her hand on my elbow. She pointed with her chin, not at a person, but at a section of the grimy tiled wall near a gum-encrusted bench. “A stain.”

To my regular eyes, it was just a darker patch of grime. But when I let my focus soften, the way she’d taught me that morning (“Look *through* the world, not at it”), I saw it. A shimmer, like heat haze, in the shape of a hunched figure. A feeling of profound, weary waiting seeped from it.

“What is it?”

“An echo. Not a ghost—a person’s intense emotion that got stuck here, like gum on a shoe. This one… loneliness. The deep kind. Someone waited here for a love who never came, day after day, until the feeling wore a groove in the air.” She pulled a small, clear vial of salt mixed with something iridescent from her satchel. “Our job is to clean it. To dissolve the stain so it doesn’t attract something worse—a hungry thing that would feed on that loneliness and amplify it for everyone who passes by.”

She sprinkled the salt mixture in a pattern before the stain. She hummed a note. The shimmer wavered, then slowly dissipated, like a sigh. The air felt lighter, just a degree less oppressive.

“You try the next one,” she said.

We rode downtown, changing lines, a relentless tour of the city’s psychic grime. Tía Ife showed me a fear-echo in the echoing corridor of Port Authority, a grief-stain outside a hospital, a burst of creative energy stuck in the reading room of the main library (“We leave those—they’re good for the students”).

By the time we emerged at West 4th Street, my head was pounding. I felt raw, oversensitive, like I’d scrubbed my psyche with steel wool.

“It’s exhausting,” I gasped, leaning against a brick wall.

“It’s work,” Tía Ife said, handing me a piece of ginger candy. “You’ll build stamina. And you’ll learn to shield. Right now, you’re a sponge. Soon, you’ll be a… selectively permeable membrane.”

We walked towards Washington Square Park. The evening was cool, the arch lit against the violet sky. And then I saw it. Not a stain. Not an echo.

A woman. Or the shape of a woman. She stood under a barren tree, wearing what looked like a flowing gown from another century, her face turned upwards. She was solid, almost more solid than the living people milling around her. And she was crying silver tears that vanished before they hit the ground. A low, beautiful hum emanated from her, a sound that made my anchor-root thrum in response.

“Ife,” I whispered, grabbing her arm.

Tía Ife followed my gaze. Her body went still. “Ah,” she said, a note of deep caution in her voice. “Not an echo. A *presence*. A strong one.”

“What is she?”

“I don’t know. But she’s not stuck. She’s… planted.” Ife observed for a moment. “She’s waiting for something. Or someone. See how the energy around her is ordered, not chaotic? This is a deliberate haunting.”

“What do we do?”

“We follow the protocol. We observe. We assess. We do not engage without knowing the score.” She began to walk slowly around the perimeter of the tree, keeping a wide berth. I followed, mesmerized. The woman’s sorrow was palpable, a field of exquisite, devastating sadness that made my own lungs ache.

As we completed our circle, a man in a business suit, talking loudly on his phone, cut directly through the space where the woman stood.

He didn’t feel a thing. But *she* reacted.

Her head snapped towards him. Her silver tears stopped. Her face, beautiful and ethereal, contorted with a sudden, blazing fury. The hum turned into a high-frequency shriek that only I could hear—it felt like ice picks in my temples. She reached out a hand as the man passed.

The man stumbled. His phone flew from his hand, smashing on the pavement. He clutched his chest, his face going pale. He gasped for air, staggering to a bench.

The woman’s form flickered, satisfaction replacing fury. Then she resumed her pose, her silver tears beginning again, the beautiful hum returning as if nothing had happened.

“Shit,” Tía Ife breathed. “She’s not just sad. She’s punitive. She’s enforcing a boundary.”

“She almost killed him!”

“She gave him a shock. A warning. He violated her space.” Ife’s face was grim. “This is beyond echo-cleaning. This is a territorial spirit with agency and power. This is the kind of thing your beacon will pull you towards.”

“We have to do something.”

“We have to *understand* first. We go to the archives.”

The “archives” were Tía Rosa’s apartment in Spanish Harlem. It was the opposite of mine: ruthlessly organized, shelves groaning with books in Spanish, English, Yoruba, and languages I didn’t recognize. Jars of herbs, drawers of feathers, cabinets of curiosities. It smelled of oregano, ozone, and old paper.

Tía Lulu was already there, baking something that smelled of anise and butter. Tía Rosa was at a large table, a map of the city spread out, dotted with colored pins.

We told them what we’d seen. Rosa listened, chewing on her unlit cigarillo.

“Washington Square,” she mused. “Old land. Potter’s field. Executions. Rebellion. Lots of layers.” She got up, went to a shelf, and pulled down a heavy, leather-bound ledger. “The presence ledger. We keep notes.”

She flipped through pages filled with dense, elegant script—descriptions, sketches, dates. “A weeping woman… silver tears… late 19th century attire…” Her finger stopped. “Here. 1987. My mother’s notation. ‘*La Llorona de Washington Square? Not the river spirit. A different grief. Artist’s model? Jilted lover? Pattern: appears every 7-10 years. Responds to disrespect. Protective of her tree. Unmoving. Sorrow with teeth.*’”

“Abuela knew her?”

“She noted her. Didn’t engage. Some presences are landmarks. You don’t move a landmark. You navigate around it.”

“But she hurt someone,” I insisted.

“He walked through her,” Tía Rosa said, shrugging. “It’s rude. You wouldn’t walk through a living woman. Why is a dead one different? She enforced her dignity.”

I was stunned. “So we just let her be? What if she does more than give a shock next time?”

Tía Ife sat down, looking weary. “It’s a calculus, Valentina. If we try to move her—a spirit that strong, that rooted—it could cause a psychic backlash. It could shatter the windows for blocks. It could attract a swarm of hungry things to the rupture. Our job is balance, not heroics.”

“But what if her presence is itself an imbalance? What if her grief is toxic?”

Tía Lulu brought over a plate of cookies. “Then you talk to her. That’s my department. But you need an invitation. You can’t just barge in. You need a key.”

“A key?”

“Something she wants. Something she recognizes. A piece of her story.” Tía Rosa closed the ledger. “Your mother would say leave it alone. An usher’s way. A conductor…” She looked at me. “A conductor might try to change the song. But you have to know the melody first.”

The complication settled in the room, heavy as the rain from the night before. This wasn’t a simple stain to be cleaned. This was a sentient, powerful, sorrowful being, woven into the fabric of the city. And my new-found senses had seen her. My anchor had resonated with her. This was my first real test.

I thought of the man clutching his chest. I thought of her silver tears. I thought of the exquisite, devastating field of her sadness.

“How do I find her key?” I asked, my voice quiet.

Tía Rosa’s gold tooth flashed in a smile. “You research. You listen to the neighborhood stories. You look for patterns older than the park. You use your beacon not just to attract, but to *inquire*.” She pushed the ledger towards me. “Start here. And remember—she’s not a problem to be solved. She’s a person to be understood. Even if she’s dead.”

I opened the ledger to Abuela Celia’s neat script. The first major complication of my new life was not a monster. It was a weeping woman under a tree, a landmark of sorrow with teeth. And I, Valentina, verb-in-training, had to decide whether to navigate around her, or try to learn her song.

I touched the page, the ghost of my grandmother’s handwriting. The anchor on my workbench at home, a world away, seemed to pulse in time with my heartbeat.

The work was no longer theoretical. It was here, in ink and memory and silver tears. And it was mine to do.



---

# Chapter 4

## CHAPTER 4

The ledger was a map of grief. Not the cartography of streets and subway lines I was used to, but a topography of absence. Abuela Celia had recorded every resonance she’d ever smoothed, every tear in the neighborhood’s fabric, in a script so precise it looked etched. *Mrs. Gable, 3B. Husband lost at sea, 1942. Keeps his shaving mug. Resonance: saltwater and brass. Resolution: returned his wedding band (found in a pawn shop on 125th). Anchor: a spool of naval thread.* I ran my finger over the entries. This wasn’t accounting. It was archaeology.

My own anchor, a twisted knot of copper wire and sea-glass I’d cobbled together on my workbench, sat warm in the pocket of my jacket. Tía Rosa called it a beacon. To me, it still felt like a stolen radio, tuned to a station that only played static and whispers. I needed to learn how to ask it a question.

The first test came on a Thursday, in the basement laundry room of my building. The air was thick with the smell of cheap detergent and mildew. I was just trying to get my socks clean, a mundane act that felt like a rebellion against the cosmic ledger now open on my kitchen table. The washing machine chugged on its final spin cycle, a lopsided rhythm. *Thump-screech, thump-screech.*

Then the rhythm changed. *Thump-weep, thump-weep.*

I froze, a ball of damp socks in my hand. The sound was soft, layered under the mechanical groan. A child’s crying. It came from the far corner, near the rusted-out sink nobody used. The temperature didn’t drop. Instead, the humidity spiked, the air turning thick as broth. A scent cut through the bleach—talcum powder and spoiled milk.

I didn’t want to look. I wanted to grab my wet clothes and run. But the anchor in my pocket gave a single, clear pulse, like a plucked string. An inquiry.

“Hello?” I said, my voice swallowed by the concrete walls.

The crying hitched. In the corner, the shadows coalesced. Not into a full form, but a smudge, a knee-high impression of deep weariness. A toddler, maybe. Curled up.

*“Perdí mi oso.”* I lost my bear.

The words weren’t in the air. They were in my head, a thought that wasn’t mine, soaked in a sadness so simple and vast it made my throat ache. This wasn’t a ghost with a story. This was a ghost with a single, unfinished feeling.

I crouched down, keeping my distance. “What kind of bear?”

A flicker. The sense of brown fur, one button eye hanging by a thread. *“Suave.”* Soft.

This was a complication, but a minor one. A stain, not a sentience. Tía Rosa’s words echoed: *Balance, not heroics.* This lost thing was a tiny knot. I could try to smooth it. My hand went to my pocket, closed around the anchor. I didn’t know what to do, so I just thought, softly, toward the smudge: *It’s okay. He was soft.*

I poured that feeling into the copper and glass—warmth, recognition, a memory of comfort I didn’t actually have. The anchor grew warm. The smudge in the corner shimmered. The crying softened to a hiccup.

*“¿Aquí?”* Here?

“Not here,” I whispered aloud. “But somewhere safe. I promise.”

It was a stupid promise. I had no idea where the bear was, or if it had ever existed. But the idea of *somewhere safe* seemed to be the key. The smudge faded, the impression of weary sadness dissolving like sugar in water. The humidity dropped. The washing machine finished with a final, normal *thunk*.

I was left kneeling on the dirty floor, my heart hammering. It was a small thing. Insignificant, in the ledger of a city’s pain. But I’d done it. I’d inquired, and I’d… navigated. The anchor cooled in my hand. My first verb, however clumsy, had been conjugated.

The success was a cup of weak tea—briefly warming, mostly water. It didn’t prepare me for the market.

***

Washington Heights doesn’t have a ghost problem. It has a people problem, which is much louder and more complicated. On Saturday morning, 175th Street was a river of sound and need. I was following a pattern from the ledger, a cluster of entries from the late 80s around a single building. *Recurring resonance: burnt sugar and regret. Manifestation: cold spots on the fire escape. Status: Unresolved.*

The building was a pre-war relic sandwiched between a pharmacy and a botanica. Its fire escape was a zigzag skeleton rusting against the brick. I stood across the street, sipping terrible bodega coffee, trying to look like a woman interested in architecture. I held the anchor loosely in my coat pocket, letting my attention go soft, trying to *inquire*.

At first, just the street noise. The thump of reggaeton from a passing car. The clatter of a shopping cart. Then, a thread. A scent, cutting through the diesel and fried plantains: caramel, pushed too far, turning acrid. And under it, the tinny echo of a salsa tune from another decade.

My eyes were drawn to a third-floor window. The curtains were drawn, but in the shadow of the fire escape landing, the air wavered. A patch of November in the May morning. I focused, pushing the anchor’s question toward it. *What are you?*

The cold spot didn’t answer. It *pulsed*.

A wave of feeling hit me—not a thought, but a pure emotional weather. Sharp regret, so potent it tasted of bile. A flash of a man’s face, handsome, smiling, holding a flowers. Then the smile turning cruel. The slam of a door. The smell of burning from the kitchen, forgotten.

It was a memory, but not mine. It was an echo stuck on repeat. And my poking at it had just turned up the volume.

The cold spot on the fire escape bloomed. Frost crackled over the iron rails. The window behind it fogged over. And the feeling amplified, spilling outwards. A woman next to me at the bus stop gasped, clutching her chest, her eyes welling with sudden, unexplained tears. A kid on a scooter wobbled, his smile collapsing into a confused frown.

I’d done this. I’d agitated a wound without knowing how to dress it. This was the “swarm of hungry things” Tía Rosa warned about—not monsters, but amplified misery, leaking into the street.

“Hey. You.”

The voice was a gravelly baritone. I turned. A man stood in the doorway of the botanica. He was older, maybe sixty, with a strong, unsmiling face the color of mahogany. He wore a crisp guayabera over slacks, and his eyes, sharp as flint, were fixed on me, then on the frost-licked fire escape.

“You’re poking the bruise,” he said, not unkindly. “Stop broadcasting.”

He lifted a hand. In it was not an anchor, but a small, woven bag of dark cloth. He shook it gently, a sound like dry seeds or bones whispering together. He murmured something in Spanish too low for me to catch, then blew across the mouth of the bag toward the building.

The scent of burnt sugar didn’t vanish, but it receded, folded back into itself. The frost on the fire escape stopped spreading. The weeping woman at the bus stop wiped her eyes, looking embarrassed. The emotional wave ebbed.

He lowered the bag and pinned me with that flinty look. “You’re new. And loud. Rosa’s kid?”

“Great-niece,” I managed, my face hot.

He nodded once. “Emilio. This is my block. You don’t stir the pot on my block without asking the chef.” He jerked his head toward the door of the botanica. “Come in. You’ve already made a mess. Might as well learn how to clean it up.”

***

The botanica was a symphony of scent and intention. Bundles of sage and palo santo hung from the ceiling. Glass cases held statues of saints with earnest, painted eyes—Eleggua, Ochún, San Miguel. Jars of herbs lined the walls, labeled in a neat hand: *Abre Camino, Dominio, Siete Potencias.* It was a place of active faith, a workshop for the will. It made Tía Rosa’s book-lined apartment feel like a quiet library.

Emilio moved behind a counter cluttered with candles in glass jars. “Rosa thinks in stories. Lulu thinks in feelings. I think in systems.” He tapped his woven bag, his *chamalongo*. “That resonance up there? It’s a loop. A woman, her *canalla* boyfriend, a ruined flan. She died of a heart attack five years later, but the strongest memory got stuck to the place. It’s not her. It’s just… a recording with the volume stuck on high.”

“I made it louder,” I said, the shame fresh.

“You shone a spotlight on it. Spotlights cause heat. Heat causes expansion.” He shrugged. “You were trying to listen. That’s the first step. But you need filters. Dampers.” He reached under the counter and placed a small, smooth stone on the wood between us. It was dark grey, with a white ring around it. A *piedra imán*. “Take it. For now, keep it in your pocket with your… thing. It’ll help you listen without screaming.”

I picked up the stone. It was cool, heavier than it looked. “Why are you helping me?”

“Because Rosa asked me to keep an eye out. And because the Weeping Woman in the Park is getting stronger.” His expression darkened. “She’s not a recording. She’s a full symphony. And her music is starting to drown out other stations. Last week, a man jumped in front of the A train at 181st. He left a note saying he just couldn’t carry the sadness anymore. He’d never been depressed a day in his life.”

The air left my lungs. The man with the silver tears, her sorrow leaching into the city’s bedrock. “You know about her?”

“Everyone who works the spiritual grid on this island knows about her. Most of us avoid Morningside Park. She’s old, she’s powerful, and she’s territorial. She’s not a complication to be resolved. She’s a fact of the landscape.” He leaned forward. “But Rosa thinks you’re a conductor, not an usher. Thinks you might try to change the song. That’s a good way to get your soul tuned to a minor key forever.”

“I just… I saw her. I felt what she feels. It’s everywhere.”

“Empathy is a door,” Emilio said. “But you need to control who you let in, or you’ll be crushed in the rush.” He looked at my anchor, which I’d nervously placed on the counter. “That’s a sensitive instrument. Good for finding signals. Bad for shielding you from them. You need an alliance. Tools. A crew.”

“A crew?”

“You think you’re the only verb in this neighborhood?” He almost smiled. “We’re not an organized bunch. More like… independent contractors. But sometimes, a job is too big for one person.” He took a deep breath. “There’s a woman. She runs a community garden on 118th, by the old church. Name’s Imani. She works with the green things, the growing memories. The Weeping Woman’s roots are in that park. If anyone knows what soil she sprung from, it’s Imani. Tell her I sent you. And tell her…” He hesitated. “Tell her the blight on the oak is spreading.”

He said it like it was a password. A shared trial I knew nothing about.

“What blight?”

“You’ll see. Consider it your audition.” He pushed the stone toward me. “Now go. And for God’s sake, learn to whisper.”

***

The community garden was a shock of green defiance between two crumbling apartment buildings. Chain-link fence crowned with morning glory vines, plots bursting with tomatoes, collards, flowers I didn’t know the names of. The air here smelled different—rich soil, compost, life fighting through concrete.

I found Imani on her knees, digging around the base of a sunflowers with hands covered in earth. She was younger than I expected, maybe late twenties, with locs piled in a bun and a face that was all fierce concentration. She didn’t look up as I approached.

“Emilio sent me,” I said, feeling absurd.

“I heard.” Her voice was low, melodic. “You’re the loud one.”

Word traveled fast on this particular network. “He said to tell you the blight on the oak is spreading.”

Her hands stilled. Slowly, she sat back on her heels and looked at me. Her eyes were the color of dark honey, and they held a deep, weary knowledge. “Come with me.”

She led me to the far corner of the garden, near the back wall of the old church. There, in a small, secluded plot, was a young oak tree. It was maybe ten feet tall, its leaves a vibrant summer green. Except they weren’t. As I got closer, I saw it. A silver sheen coated every leaf, dripping from the tips like metallic dew. It wasn’t water. It was the same liquid silver I’d seen falling from the Weeping Woman’s face. The ground around the tree’s roots was stained with it, the grass withered and grey.

“It started last month,” Imani said quietly. “Just a few leaves. Now… it’s weeping. Her sorrow is toxic to living things. It’s a grief that doesn’t fertilize. It sterilizes.”

I reached out, not to touch, but with my attention, the anchor a quiet hum in my pocket next to Emilio’s stone. I inquired, gently. The tree’s song was a muted, pained drone. Underneath it, I felt the vast, echoing thrum of the Weeping Woman’s power, a deep root reaching up from the park blocks away, tangling with the tree’s own spirit.

“She’s connected to everything green there,” Imani said. “The park is her body. This tree… it’s like a capillary. It’s drawing her poison.”

“Can you save it?”

“I can try. But it’s a symptom. The disease is her.” She wiped her hands on her jeans, leaving muddy streaks. “Emilio thinks you want to try something stupid. Noble, but stupid.”

“I want to understand her song,” I said, repeating Tía Rosa’s words. “I need to find her key.”

Imani studied me for a long moment. “The stories say she appears to those who are about to drown in their own sadness. She doesn’t kill them. She just… shows them the depth of the water. And some people can’t swim back.” She knelt again, placing her palms on the earth near the blighted roots. “I can’t fight her. My work is healing, growth. But I can try to protect the green things. And I can tell you where to dig.”

“Dig?”

“For her story. The oldest version.” She closed her eyes, her voice dropping to a whisper. “There’s a man. He used to be a history professor at City College. Now he’s just a drunk who lives in the archives of his own mind. Name’s Silas. He knows the bones of this island, the stories buried under the pavement. If anyone has her key, it’s him. He drinks at a bar called The Final Stop on 110th and Broadway. But be careful. He trades in stories, but he’s paid for them in pieces of his sanity.”

An archivist of sorrows. It fit. “Will you help me?” I asked. “With the tree? With… whatever comes next?”

Imani opened her eyes. She looked at the silver-dripping leaves, then at my face, raw with a determination I didn’t fully feel. “This tree is my ally,” she said finally. “If you are serious about helping it, then we have a shared trial. But my alliance is with the living. You go into the dead woman’s heart, you go alone. I’ll hold the line here.”

It wasn’t a full partnership. It was a treaty. A defined border of trust. It was more than I had an hour ago. I nodded.

“Thank you.”

“Don’t thank me yet,” she said, turning back to the blighted oak. Her hands began to move over the soil, a gentle, rhythmic motion. A soft, green-gold light, faint as a firefly’s glow, emanated from her palms, pushing back against the silver stain. It was a slow, stubborn fight. “Go find Silas. And remember—some keys unlock doors. Others just show you how deep the lock goes.”

***

The Final Stop was the kind of bar that existed in perpetual twilight. It was barely afternoon, but inside, the air was brown and timeless, smelling of stale beer, old wood, and the faint, sweet decay of forgotten things. A few regulars hunched over their glasses like monuments to regret.

He was in the back booth, a mountain of rumpled tweed and white hair. Silas. A bottle of cheap rum sat between him and a precarious stack of notebooks and loose papers covered in frantic, spidery handwriting. He was arguing with the empty air across from him.

“No, you’re missing the point entirely! The Lenape didn’t *vanish*. They were paved over! The streets are a palimpsest, you fool, and we’re writing our grocery lists on top of a funeral dirge!”

I slid into the booth opposite him. He didn’t stop, just shifted his furious, bloodshot gaze to me. “And who are you? Another developer? A ghost-tour charlatan? My ex-wife’s lawyer?”

“Emilio sent me. And Imani.”

The names were a spell. The fire in his eyes banked, replaced by a sharp, assessing intelligence. He took a slow drink from his glass. “The botanist and the brujo. That’s a serious summons. You must be in deep water, girl.”

“The Weeping Woman in Morningside Park.”

A profound stillness settled over him. All the performative anger drained away, leaving something old and cold. “La Llorona de Morningside,” he murmured. “You don’t want her story. Her story wants you. It’s a sticky thing. Gets in your dreams.”

“I need to understand her. I need her key.”

He laughed, a dry, crackling sound. “Her key? She’s not a locked diary. She’s a landslide. You don’t *understand* a landslide. You get out of its path.” He leaned forward, his breath a cloud of rum and sorrow. “But you’re not here for wisdom. You’re here for data. Fine. A trade. I give you the oldest song. You give me…” His eyes flicked to the anchor I’d unconsciously placed on the table. “A taste of that. A new frequency. My archives are always accepting donations.”

It was a violation. Tía Rosa’s warning echoed. But the silver blight on the oak, the man in front of the train—they were a drumbeat. I needed the melody. I nodded, once.

Silas’s smile was a gash. He reached into his stack of papers, pulling out a single, brittle sheet. It wasn’t a photocopy. It was a handwritten page from a 19th-century diary, the ink faded to brown.

“Read it aloud,” he whispered. “And hold onto your soul.”

I took the page. The handwriting was a feminine script, elegant but strained. I began to read, my voice barely a whisper in the dark booth.

*“October 12, 1888. The park is complete, they say. A masterpiece of Olmsted’s design. They brought in tons of soil, planted mature trees, all to hide the scars of the fighting. My Henry is gone. Not to war, but to the fever that took so many in the shantytown they cleared to build this ‘paradise.’ They promised him work moving the earth. The earth took him instead.*

*“I go to the great oak where we pledged ourselves. It is the only thing they left standing. I weep there. I weep until I have no tears, and then I weep silver, for my heart has turned to metal with the weight of it. I hear the other women too, in the wind. The Lenape mothers who wept for children taken by sickness and sword. The Irish mothers who wept for boys buried in the railroad cuts. This ground is a sponge for tears. We are the water it cannot hold.*

*“I will weep here until the park is dust. I will be the landmark they did not intend. My grief is my deed. This tree is my title.”*

I stopped. The words hung in the smoky air, alive and terrible. This wasn’t just a ghost story. It was a protest. A property claim staked in eternal sorrow. She wasn’t lost. She was *occupying*.

Silas was watching me, his eyes gleaming. “Her name was Elara Vane. Not a poor immigrant. A middle-class woman who loved the wrong man and watched this ‘sanitized’ park be built over his grave. Her grief attached to the older grief in the land—the layers of it. She didn’t become a ghost. She became a *conglomerate*. A focal point. That’s her power. She doesn’t just feel her own sadness. She amplifies the buried sadness of the place itself. She’s a living—well, not living—monument to every tear ever shed on that ground.”

The nature of her plan wasn’t conquest. It was *revelation*. She was making the hidden pain of the city visible, toxic, undeniable. She was forcing everyone to taste the history they walked over.

“The key,” I breathed. “It’s not an object. It’s Henry. Her husband.”

Silas shook his head, a slow, sad movement. “Henry is dust. It’s the *promise*. The promise made under that oak. The promise broken by death, by progress, by time. You can’t give her back her husband. But can you acknowledge the promise? Can you speak to the betrayal that the very land represents?” He reached out a trembling hand, hovering over my anchor. “My payment.”

I felt a surge of protectiveness. But a deal was a deal. I placed my hand over the copper and glass, opened my senses just a crack, and let a thread of my own resonance flow—not the deep stuff, just the surface: the anxiety of the city, the determined hope of Imani’s garden, the cool logic of Emilio’s systems. A modern frequency.

Silas’s fingers didn’t touch it. He just absorbed the vibration, his eyes fluttering closed. A single tear traced through the grime on his cheek. “Fascinating,” he whispered. “So much noise. So much… life.” He looked exhausted, fed. “Now go. You have your melody. It’s a funeral march. Good luck changing the tune.”

I stood, the diary page clutched in my hand. I felt heavier, as if the story had entered me and added its weight to my bones. I knew her name. I knew her grievance. I knew the awesome, terrifying scope of her power—she was a conductor, too, in her way, orchestrating the hidden sorrow of an entire city.

As I pushed open the door of The Final Stop, blinking in the late afternoon sun, the anchor in my pocket gave a sudden, violent twitch. Not a pulse of inquiry. A jolt of alarm.

I looked down the street, toward the distant green seam of Morningside Park. The air above the tree line shimmered, not with heat, but with a cold, silver light. A column of distilled weeping, visible even in daylight, rising like a beacon.

She was no longer just a landmark. She was broadcasting. And the signal was getting stronger. My research was over. The direct clash wasn’t coming.

It was here.



---

# Chapter 5

## CHAPTER 5

The silver light over Morningside Park didn’t flicker. It bled. It seeped into the afternoon haze, turning the sun into a dull coin behind a sheet of dirty gauze. The anchor in my pocket was a live wire now, humming a single, sustained note of warning that vibrated in my molars. I started walking. The city sounds—the jackhammers, the sirens, the bass from a passing car—didn’t fade. They just became irrelevant, like someone had turned down the volume on everything but that silent, weeping frequency.

I found Imani and Emilio at the community garden, but they were already standing at the rusted gate, looking north. Imani had a trowel in one hand, dirt crusted under her nails. Emilio’s tablet was dark in his grip.

“You see it,” Imani said. It wasn’t a question.

“Silas gave me a name,” I said. My voice sounded thin, scraped raw by the static still clinging to my throat from The Final Stop. “Eleanor. Her husband was Henry. They made a promise under an oak tree where the park’s concert shell is now. He died. The city took the tree, built over their dream. She’s not haunting a place. She’s haunting the broken promise.”

Emilio blinked, processing. “So the ‘key’ is… an apology?”

“It’s an acknowledgment,” I said, echoing Silas’s weary wisdom. “The land remembers the betrayal. She’s become its conductor. All the grief, the displacement… she’s tuning it, focusing it. That,” I pointed to the silver column, now pulsing faintly like a sick heart, “is a spiritual broadcast. And it’s getting a hell of a signal boost.”

Imani hefted her trowel, then tossed it into a wheelbarrow with a metallic clang. “Then we go acknowledge. We go listen.”

“It’s not a therapy session,” I said. “Silas called it a funeral march. We’re walking into the heart of a dirge.”

“We have a plan?” Emilio asked, powering up his tablet. The screen showed a topographic map of the park, overlaid with his earlier EMF readings. A massive, swirling vortex of activity was centered on the concert shell.

“We have a theory,” I corrected. “And I have a ghost in my pocket who thinks cheap mezcal is for amateurs. So, no. Not a plan.”

But we went. The three of us, a pathetic procession: the thief, the gardener, the tech. The seeker, the nurturer, the logician. We walked up the hill, the silver light growing from a stain on the horizon to a dome that swallowed the park’s northern end. The air changed. The wet-pavement smell of the city was gone, replaced by the damp, mineral scent of deep soil and the faint, sweet decay of fallen leaves. It was the smell of something old, and something buried.

We entered the park at 116th. The usual soundtrack—kids on the playground, couples on blankets, the distant thwock of tennis balls—was absent. Not silent. Replaced. A low, polyphonic sigh moved through the branches of the pin oaks. It wasn’t wind. It was exhalation. The anchor’s hum climbed an octave, harmonizing uncomfortably with the sigh.

“The boundary,” Emilio murmured, his eyes on his tablet. His screen was a riot of crimson spikes. “Readings are off the scale. It’s not a hotspot. It’s a… territory.”

We passed the empty chess tables. Past the deserted basketball courts. The silver light wasn’t illuminating; it was leaching color, turning the world into a mercury-glass negative. My skin prickled with cold. Imani walked with her hands slightly out, as if feeling for a texture in the air.

“Can you feel it?” she whispered. “The grief’s not uniform. It’s layered. Like strata.”

I opened my senses, just a crack. It hit me like a wave of nausea. I wasn’t feeling one woman’s sorrow. I was feeling the park’s memory of it. The sharp, personal agony of Eleanor’s loss was the bedrock. But layered atop it was the generic misery of a thousand bad days, the echoes of lost dogs and broken hearts and missed opportunities that had seeped into the grass. And on top of *that*, a newer, sharper stratum: the communal anger of displacement, the rumble of bulldozers, the phantom scent of crushed clapboard and uprooted rose bushes from the homes that were cleared to make this pastoral fantasy for the rich.

Eleanor wasn’t just broadcasting her cry. She was conducting a symphony of sorrow, each movement a different era of loss.

“It’s a spiritual archive,” I breathed, shutting the sense down before it overwhelmed me. “She’s the curator.”

We reached the crest of the hill, the vast lawn sloping down to the concert shell. The shell was a stone half-dome, a relic of a more grandiose vision for the park. Before it, the ground was paved in concentric arcs of flagstone. Or it should have been.

The flagstones were gone. In their place was a massive, gnarled oak tree, its branches spread wide and leafless against the silver-lit sky. Its roots buckled the earth, tearing through the modern pavement. It was solid. It was impossibly there. And at its base, on a worn wooden bench that also hadn’t existed ten minutes ago, sat a woman.

She was turned away from us, a silhouette in a long, old-fashioned coat, her hair pinned up. She was very still. The column of light originated from her, from the space between her bowed shoulders and the tree.

This was it. The edge of her territory. The source.

Imani stopped. “How do we approach a landmark?”

“Carefully,” Emilio said, his voice tight. “And with extreme prejudice to our sanity.”

My hand was around the anchor in my pocket. The copper was so cold it burned. *Abuela?* I thought, sending the impulse inward. *Any last-minute advice on protocol for confronting a weeping goddess?*

A faint scent of gardenias. A feeling of shrugged shoulders. *You’re the one who walked into the broadcast, mija. Turn the dial yourself.*

Right.

We started down the hill. The sighing in the trees focused, becoming a whisper that skittered across the stones around the resurrected oak. It resolved into words, not in my ears, but in the back of my teeth.

*…promised… here… always…*

Each step was heavier. The air grew thick, resistant. Imani was breathing in short, sharp pants. Emilio’s tablet screen went black and stayed black.

We were ten yards from the bench when the ground shifted.

Not metaphorically. The earth in front of us *heaved*, like a sleeping beast turning over. Soil and shattered flagstone erupted. Figures pulled themselves up from the dirt. They weren’t human. They were rough-hewn, shaped from compacted earth, twigs, and crushed brick. Their faces were smooth indentations, their bodies bulky and slow. Guardians. Manifestations of the land’s defense mechanism.

There were three of them. They stood between us and the bench, a wall of animated terrain.

“Okay,” Emilio said, backpedaling. “New obstacle.”

Imani didn’t retreat. She stepped forward, planting her boots firmly. “They’re not attackers. They’re… askers.”

She was right. The figures didn’t advance. They simply stood, blocking the way. Their blank faces turned toward each of us in turn. The whisper in the air changed.

*…worth…?…worth…?…worth…?*

“It’s a test,” I said, the realization dawning. “She’s not just angry. She’s *discerning*. She wants to know if we’re worth listening to. If our pain is real enough to earn an audience.”

The figure in front of Imani shifted. Vines, thin and quick as snakes, sprouted from its earthen shoulders. They didn’t strike. They extended toward her, quivering.

Imani held out her hands, palms up. The vines touched her skin, coiled gently around her wrists. She closed her eyes. I saw it then—the memory she offered. Not the big one, not the loss of her brother, but a small, sharp one: the first time she’d come to this park after moving to the city, feeling utterly rootless, and had stolen a handful of seeds from a dying flower bed. The desperate, irrational hope that she could make something grow in a pot on a fire escape. The personal promise to a piece of earth that wasn’t hers.

The vine-figure shuddered. A single, perfect morning glory bloomed from its chest, a burst of blue in the monochrome gloom. It stepped aside.

The figure before Emilio changed differently. Its surface smoothed, hardened, took on a metallic sheen. Circuits, faintly glowing, etched themselves across its torso. A low, logical hum emitted from it. A question of data.

Emilio swallowed. He tucked his dead tablet under his arm and reached out, placing his hand flat on the figure’s cool, metallic chest. He didn’t offer a memory of emotion. He offered a *pattern*. The clean, beautiful logic of a system that works, the grief he felt when that logic was corrupted by chaos, by the unpredictable, messy pain of the world that his code could never fix. The sorrow of a mind that seeks order in a universe of entropy.

The metallic figure’s hum shifted pitch, harmonizing with his. It stepped aside.

Then they both turned. And the third figure, the one in front of me, focused.

It didn’t sprout vines or circuits. It became less distinct, more like a pillar of mist and shadow. It approached, and the cold intensified. The whisper was directly for me.

*…thief… taker… empty vessel… what do you offer?*

It saw me. It saw my method—the skimming of resonance, the borrowing of pain and memory that wasn’t mine to fund my own survival. It saw the hollows Abuela’s ghost sometimes filled. My currency was stolen grief.

My hand was still clenched around the anchor. I could give it a thread of something I’d collected. The anxiety from the bodega. The determined hope from Imani’s garden. Silas’s weary hunger. But that was the old way. The cheap mezcal.

The figure loomed. The cold was in my bones.

I let go of the anchor. I opened my empty hands.

“I offer my own silence,” I said, my voice cracking. “I listen to the dead because the living never made sense. I take echoes because my own voice never seemed enough. That’s my loss. That’s my emptiness. I’m a thief because I’ve always been afraid I had nothing of my own to give.”

It was the barest truth. The core of it. The spiritual seeking that had always been a kind of running away.

The mist-figure didn’t step aside. It *flowed* around me. For a second, I was inside it, and I felt the vast, cool archive of the park’s sorrows, the immense weight of Eleanor’s curated grief. It was an ocean, and I was a single, empty cup. Then it was behind me, reforming.

The way was clear.

The woman on the bench hadn’t moved.

We approached, the three of us, stepping into the profound chill of the oak’s shadow. I could see details now. The fine wool of her coat. The gray streaking her dark, pinned hair. The way her shoulders trembled, not with sobs, but with the effort of containing the broadcast.

“Eleanor,” I said.

The silver light flickered. The whispering sigh in the trees died.

Slowly, she turned her head.

She wasn’t a rotted corpse or a spectral cliché. She was a woman in late middle age, her face lined with a profound, tired sadness. Her eyes were the color of a winter sky, and they held the same distant, shimmering light that filled the air. They were focused on something miles, or decades, away.

“You hear the music,” she said. Her voice was the whisper in the trees given clarity. It was the creak of a branch, the trickle of water over stone.

“We hear it,” Imani said softly.

“It’s the only thing left,” Eleanor said, turning her gaze back to the space under the oak. “He promised. We would build a house here. A life. Watch the seasons turn from this spot. The city took the tree. Time took Henry. But promises… promises have a weight. They sink into the ground. They become part of the geology.” She finally looked at me, and the full force of her attention was like standing in a spotlight made of ice. “You carry a weight. I can feel it. You understand.”

“I understand stolen things,” I said. “Broken things. I’m sorry about your tree. I’m sorry about Henry.”

She smiled, a thin, heartbreaking movement. “Sorry is a breath. It scatters. The promise is a stone.” She lifted a hand, gesturing to the park around us, to the city beyond. “They are all stones. Every dream that didn’t survive the breaking ground. Every love that didn’t survive the changing season. I hold them. I give them a voice. Otherwise, they are just… noise. Forgotten.”

That’s when I saw it. The true scope. She wasn’t just mourning her husband. She had *become* the memorial. A living, weeping monument to every loss this piece of earth had ever absorbed. Her spiritual seeking had gone so deep it had fused her with the land’s pain. She wasn’t a ghost haunting a place. She was the place’s grief, given a shape.

“You’re tuning it,” Emilio said, awe in his voice. “You’re not just crying. You’re composing.”

“Someone must,” she said. “Or the weight will sink the whole island.”

And I knew, with a certainty that turned my stomach to lead, what Silas had meant. This was a funeral march. A beautiful, terrible, necessary song. To stop it wasn’t to bring peace. It was to impose a new kind of silence. A forgetting.

“What do you want?” I asked, the question feeling useless as soon as it left my lips.

Eleanor looked at the space under the oak. “I want the promise kept. I want the tree. I want the man. I want the future that was mine.” She looked back at me. “You cannot give that. No one can. So I will play the song of its absence. Until the stones themselves wear away from the vibration.”

A deal. A transaction. That was my language. But the price was impossible.

“There has to be another way,” Imani said, stepping forward. “A garden grows from decay. New life from old pain. You don’t have to be the tombstone. You could be the soil.”

Eleanor’s winter-eyes considered her. “You grow flowers in stolen dirt. It is a brave, futile gesture. I am not brave. I am tired. I am the dirt. And I remember what was stolen from me.”

The anchor in my pocket gave a sudden, sharp pulse. Not of alarm. Of… opportunity. A nudge.

*The cheap stuff, mija.*

An idea, terrible and fragile, clicked into place. It wasn’t a solution. It was a blasphemy.

“You can’t have your promise back,” I said, my voice hardening. “But you don’t have to be its only witness.”

Eleanor’s gaze sharpened. The cold intensified.

“You’ve made it a monument. Let us… let us *share* the burden. You’re conducting an archive. Let us… add a volume. A new one. Not of loss. Of… acknowledgment.”

“Your breath-words,” she dismissed.

“No,” I said, pulling the anchor from my pocket. The copper and glass glinted dully in the silver light. “A recording.”

Imani and Emilio stared at me. I didn’t look at them. I was watching Eleanor. A flicker of confusion in the timeless sorrow.

“You want the promise remembered? We will remember it. Not as a ghost story. Not as a static thing. We will take your melody… and we will add our own counterpoint. We will weave it into the living city’s noise. We will make the people who walk here feel the echo of your Henry, of your oak, in the midst of their own lives. The promise won’t be a stone sinking the island. It will be a thread in the fabric. Still there. But not alone. Not crushing.”

It was the most insane thing I’d ever proposed. I was asking a goddess of grief to let me remix her requiem.

The air went perfectly, utterly still. The silver light seemed to contract around us.

“You would… dilute it,” Eleanor whispered. There was no anger. Just a profound, horrified curiosity.

“I would *connect* it,” I said, pressing my advantage, my heart hammering against my ribs. “Isolation is what makes the pain absolute. You’ve built a cathedral of sorrow. I’m asking you to open the doors. Let some of the street noise in. It’s a different kind of remembering.”

I held out the anchor. “This holds echoes. Let me take one thread of your song. The purest thread. The memory of the promise under the oak. Not the breaking. The making. Let me take that, and I will weave it into the static of 103rd and Amsterdam. I will plant it in Imani’s garden. I will let it run through Emilio’s circuits. It won’t be forgotten. It will be… repurposed.”

I was a thief, after all. Offering the ultimate heist: not to steal her grief, but to redistribute it.

Eleanor stared at the device in my hand. For a long moment, there was only the vast, listening silence of the park. She looked at the empty space under her oak. She looked at her own hands, pale and translucent.

“The making of the promise,” she repeated. A tear, solid and silver as mercury, traced down her cheek. It hit the ground with a soft *ping*, and a single, tiny blade of grass, impossibly green, sprouted where it fell.

She nodded, once. A slow, seismic movement.

“Play your counterpoint,” she said.

And she opened her hands.

The silver light didn’t vanish. It *unspooled*. A single, brilliant filament, brighter than the rest, peeled away from the column above her. It was the color of a wedding band, of a new leaf in sun, of a vow spoken in good faith. It snaked through the air toward my anchor.

I didn’t activate it. I just held it up, open and receptive.

The filament touched the glass. The anchor didn’t vibrate. It *sang*. A clear, pure, major-key note that cut through the dirge. It was the sound of “yes,” of “always,” of a future believed in.

I felt it enter, not as a stolen resonance, but as a bestowed one. A gift of a memory that was not mine. It settled into the copper coils, warm and alive and devastatingly hopeful.

The moment it was captured, the world shattered.

Not the oak, not the bench. The *expectation*. The spiritual seeking I’d been engaged in—the lonely, transactional hunt for truths to use as currency—it cracked open.

Eleanor didn’t fade. She… changed. The column of light dimmed, not to darkness, but to a soft, pearlescent glow. The crushing weight of the sorrow lifted by a fraction. She was still a monument, but now there was a window in the wall. She looked at me, and for the first time, her eyes held something besides distant grief.

A question.

The oak tree began to blur at the edges. The flagstones reasserted themselves, ghostly at first, then solid. The bench was just a bench again. Eleanor was still there, but she was translucent, a watermark on the air.

She looked at the space where her tree had been, then at the anchor in my hand, now glowing with its own, gentle, gold-and-silver light.

“A thread in the fabric,” she whispered. Then she was gone.

The ordinary sounds of the park rushed back in—a dog barking, a distant siren, the laugh of a child. The late afternoon sun, the real one, broke through the clouds over the Hudson, painting the concert shell in warm, ordinary light.

We stood there, breathing hard, as if we’d just surfaced from deep water.

Imani touched my arm. “Did we… help her?”

“I don’t know,” I said honestly, staring at the singing anchor in my palm. It felt different. Lighter, but more significant. “I think we changed the song. I don’t know if that’s better.”

Emilio’s tablet chirped back to life. He looked at the screen, his brow furrowed. “The massive EMF vortex is… dispersed. It’s fractured into a hundred smaller, weaker signals scattered across the park. Like you said. A distribution.”

We had done it. We had faced the ordeal, passed the test, offered a sacrifice of ego, and altered the spiritual landscape. We hadn’t defeated a ghost. We’d negotiated with a force of nature. We’d turned a monologue into, potentially, a conversation.

But as we turned to walk back down the hill, the anchor gave one last, subtle pulse. A notification.

I focused on it, opening the sense just a hair.

The pure thread of Eleanor’s promise was there, beautiful and safe. But woven around it, responding to it, were other threads. Fainter, stranger. They had been attracted to the new signal. A buzzing, inquisitive resonance from the direction of the Columbia campus. A slow, deep, mineral pulse from the bedrock near the Cloisters. A skittering, playful echo from the abandoned tunnel near the river.

The anchor wasn’t just holding one memory. It had become a beacon. A tuning fork struck with a new frequency, and it was ringing out, calling to other hidden melodies in the city’s spiritual static.

We hadn’t ended the symphony. We’d changed the key. And every musician in the invisible orchestra had just lifted their head, instruments poised, wondering who was playing the new tune.

The seeking wasn’t over. It had just gotten a whole lot more complicated.



---

# Chapter 6

## CHAPTER 6

The buzzing from the Columbia campus resolved into a headache three days later. It wasn’t a sound, not exactly. It was the psychic equivalent of a dentist’s drill—a high, insistent whine that vibrated behind my eyes and made my teeth ache. The anchor in my jacket pocket, usually a quiet, cool weight, hummed in sympathetic irritation. It didn’t like the new neighbors.

Imani called it “spiritual tinnitus.” She’d been feeling it too, a pressure at the base of her skull. Emilio’s gear had gone haywire, picking up erratic pulses that looked less like ghostly signatures and more like aggressive, intelligent sonar. Something was pinging us. Something had heard the anchor’s new frequency and was not just listening, but calling back.

We tracked the source to a gothic revival building on campus, all scowling gargoyles and leaded glass. The Department of Medieval Studies. The air here didn’t smell of gardenias or wet leaves. It smelled of ozone, old paper, and a sharp, coppery tang like blood on a penny.

“This is a bad idea,” Emilio muttered, staring at the arched oak door. His tablet screen was a constellation of angry red dots clustered behind it. “The EMF isn’t residual. It’s structured. Patterned. It’s not a memory. It’s a mind.”

“That’s what we’re here for,” I said, my voice tighter than I intended. The anchor pulsed, a warning throb. “It’s pinging the anchor. It knows we’re here. We walk away now, it just follows the signal home.”

Imani squared her shoulders. “So we negotiate. Like with Eleanor.”

But this wasn’t Eleanor’s gentle, tragic longing. This buzz was all interrogation. It wanted to know what we were, how we’d changed the song, and what we planned to do with the instrument. I pushed the door open.

The inside was a cathedral of knowledge gone feral. The buzzing wasn’t in the air; it was in the books. Thousands of them, lining shelves that reached a shadowy ceiling. But they weren’t silent. Their spines seemed to thrum. A faint, ghostly light, the color of a bruise, emanated from between their pages, illuminating drifting motes of dust that swirled in deliberate, non-random patterns. Equations. Sigils. Sentences in dead languages.

In the center of the room, standing before a massive stone fireplace cold for a century, was a man. Or the memory of one. He was translucent, etched in the same bruised light, wearing the robes of a late 19th-century scholar. His head was bent over a phantom book in his hands. His lips moved soundlessly, endlessly.

“Professor Alistair Finch,” Imani breathed, reading the tarnished brass nameplate on the lectern. “Noted historian. Died in this building, 1898. Stroke, mid-lecture.”

As she spoke, the professor’s head snapped up. He had no eyes, just pools of that same bruised light. The buzzing in the room focused, sharpened, becoming a voice that didn’t enter through our ears but drilled directly into the meat of our brains.

*INTERLOCUTORS. YOU HAVE THE INSTRUMENT. YOU HAVE ALTERED THE ESTABLISHED PARAMETERS OF LOCALIZED HISTORICAL RESONANCE. PRESENT YOUR METHODOLOGY.*

The words were flat, demanding, devoid of anything resembling emotion. This wasn’t a ghost haunted by a feeling. This was an intellect haunted by its own unfinished thesis.

“We helped a spirit find peace,” I said, forcing the words past the pressure in my skull. “We listened. We changed her song.”

*PEACE IS A CESSATION OF DATA. A NULL SET. YOUR ACTION CREATED A DISPERSAL PATTERN, A FRACTALIZATION OF ENERGY. THIS IS NOT RESOLUTION. THIS IS CHAOS.* He—it—drifted closer. The phantom book dissolved. Its hands, now just bundles of crackling energy, reached out. *YOU HAVE INITIATED AN UNCONTROLLED EXPERIMENT. THE INSTRUMENT MUST BE STUDIED. CONTAINED. THE VARIABLES MUST BE CONTROLLED.*

This was the fear. Not a monster, but a librarian. A curator of the unseen who saw our seeking not as a journey, but as a contamination of his pristine archive. His greatest fear was noise in his signal. And we were a symphony of static.

“It’s not a tool,” Imani said, her voice strained but clear. “It’s a connection. It’s not for you to contain.”

*INCORRECT. ALL PHENOMENA ARE FOR CATALOGUING.* The buzzing intensified. The books on the shelves glowed brighter. Phantom pages began to tear themselves free, swirling around us in a cyclone of spectral text. *YOU ARE PHENOMENA. YOU WILL BE CATALOGUED.*

The attack wasn’t physical. It was epistemological. The pages didn’t cut skin; they cut understanding. One brushed my arm, and a jolt of pure, disorienting information flooded me—the entire lineage of a minor Frankish duke, dates, battles, names of horses, all in a half-second scream of data. I stumbled, my own memories scrambling. I forgot my zip code. I forgot Abuela Celia’s face for one terrifying, hollow second.

Imani cried out, clutching her head. “It’s… rewriting. Stop!”

Emilio fumbled with his gear, but a sheaf of pages wrapped around his tablet. The screen flashed with gibberish, then went dark, smoking slightly. “It’s frying the circuits! It’s treating technology as a rival text!”

Finch advanced, a storm of aggressive scholarship. *SUBMIT YOUR DATA. THE INSTRUMENT. YOUR MEMORIES OF THE EVENT. YOUR CORRUPTIVE METHODOLOGY. SUBMIT.*

I fumbled for the anchor. It was burning hot now, vibrating like a plucked string. I tried to push back with Eleanor’s song, the clean thread of her promise. But Finch’s buzzing was a firewall of pure logic. It dissected the emotion, labeled it “sentimental bias,” and filed it away. It couldn’t be reasoned with. It could only categorize.

“Run!” I yelled, shoving Imani toward the door.

We burst out into the cool evening air, but the buzzing didn’t stop. It followed us, a swarm of invisible hornets in our minds. The pages of data were gone, but the effect remained—a scrambling, a loss of cohesion. I couldn’t remember the order of the subway lines. Emilio kept staring at his hands as if he’d forgotten their function.

We ran, not to the subway, but instinctively downtown, toward the only place that had ever felt like a refuge in this war of whispers: the bodega on 103rd.

***

We collapsed in the back aisle, between the dried chilies and the bags of Goya beans. The familiar smells of cumin and floor cleaner were a balm. The buzzing receded here, blocked by some older, quieter magic. But the damage was done.

Imani sat with her back against a shelf of canned peppers, her eyes closed, tears cutting clean tracks through the dust on her cheeks. “I can’t… I can’t remember the first line of my favorite poem. I know I love it. I can feel the shape of the love. But the words are gone.”

Emilio was methodically taking apart his dead tablet, his fingers trembling. “He didn’t just attack us. He… indexed us. Took copies. My schematics, my notes on the EMF patterns… they’re not secrets anymore. They’re entries in his ledger.”

I felt hollowed out. The anchor in my hand was quiet, cold. Dormant. Finch’s assault had forced it to retract, to protect its core. We had faced a fear, alright. Not of death, but of erasure. Of being footnoted in some spectral dissertation, our souls reduced to data points. We had tried to negotiate, and we had been comprehensively, intellectually defeated.

This was the death. Not of the body, but of the quest. The seeking had led us to a force that sought to end all seeking, to file it away on a shelf. What was the point of listening to the city’s songs if a mad librarian could just silence the singers and check out their memories?

“He’s right, in a way,” I said, my voice a rasp. “We unleashed chaos. We rang a dinner bell. We thought we were starting a conversation, but we just announced a free-for-all. And the first thing that answered wants to put the whole city in a card catalog.”

Imani opened her eyes. They were red-rimmed, but fierce. “So we give up? Let him have it? Let him have *us*?”

“What’s left to fight with?” Emilio gestured with a stripped screwdriver. “He eats technology. He eats memory. He eats *stories*. What do we have that he can’t just… footnote?”

The answer came not from within me, but from the air. A scent cut through the bodega smells of spice and plastic—gardenias and clove cigarettes.

“You have cheap mezcal and a bad attitude, *mija*. That’s always been enough.”

Abuela Celia stood by the dairy case, her mint-green housecoat glowing faintly against the stacked quarts of milk. She looked more solid than before, the pom-poms on her slippers almost vibrant.

I was too defeated for surprise. “He’ll just catalog you too, Abuela. He’ll call you ‘Folk Belief, Appendix B.’”

She snorted, a sound like dry leaves. “That *cabeza de chorlito*? That pinched-up professor? He thinks because he died with a book in his hand, he owns the library.” She drifted closer, and for the first time, I felt not cold, but a prickling, vibrant warmth from her, like the sun on a brick wall. “He knows words. He knows facts. But he doesn’t know *stories*. And he certainly doesn’t know *this* neighborhood.”

A new kind of understanding began to dawn, not in my mind, but in my gut. A memory, not stolen, but given back. I remembered Abuela not at her end, sick and frail, but in her prime, standing on this very block, hands on her hips, arguing with the landlord in a torrent of Spanish and Spanglish so potent it seemed to warp the air around her. She hadn’t just lived here. She had *argued* this place into existence, had defined its boundaries with her will.

“The anchor,” I whispered. “It’s not a radio. It’s a… a neighborhood watch. It tunes into the local frequency.”

Abuela Celia’s grin was all mischief. “Now you’re cooking with gas, *niña*. That professor, he’s a squatter. Moved into a building he doesn’t understand, on land that never agreed to hold him. He’s loud. He’s rude. He’s putting up shelves in a place that belongs to the street.”

Imani was staring, the lost look in her eyes replaced by a dawning fire. “We can’t fight his story. It’s too strong, too logical.”

“So we don’t fight it,” I said, the plan forming not as a strategy, but as a feeling. A deep, territorial impulse. “We drown it out. We change the channel back to what this land remembers. Back to *our* static.”

Emilio looked from me to my ghostly grandmother. “How? A noise complaint?”

“A block party,” I said, standing up. The hollow feeling was filling with a defiant, buzzing energy of our own.
